Now E = (a + t_E Δ, b + t_E dy). Similarly, t_F = -2 ((A-N)·v)/Q. But we need (A-N)·v = (a-c, b)·v = (a-c)Δ + b dy = (a-c)Δ - (a+r)(Δ+r) because b dy = - (a+r)(Δ+r). So:

(a-c)Δ - (a+r)(Δ+r) = (a-c)Δ - (a+r)Δ - (a+r)r = Δ(a-c - a - r) - r(a+r) = Δ(-c - r) - r(a+r) = -(c+r)Δ - r(a+r).

Thus (A-N)·v = - (c+r)Δ - r(a+r) = -[(c+r)Δ + r(a+r)].

Then t_F = -2 * ( (A-N)·v ) / Q = -2 * ( -[(c+r)Δ + r(a+r)] ) / Q = 2[(c+r)Δ + r(a+r)]/Q.

So t_F = 2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q.

Note: Δ = p - a. p = (c+R-r)/2. So we can express everything in terms of a, b, c, r, R. But a is expressed in terms of c, r, R. So perhaps we can eliminate a and b in favor of c, r, R. Since b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So we have a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Then b^2 = r^2 - ((c^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4c^2) = (4c^2r^2 - (c^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4c^2). The numerator is: (2cr)^2 - (c^2+r^2-R^2)^2 = (2cr - (c^2+r^2-R^2))(2cr + (c^2+r^2-R^2)). But we know that circles intersect, so that expression is positive. We'll keep it as b^2 = (4c^2r^2 - (c^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4c^2). So b = (1/(2c)) * sqrt(4c^2r^2 - (c^2+r^2-R^2)^2). That is a typical expression.

Now we can compute Q = Δ^2 + ((a+r)^2(Δ+r)^2)/b^2.

So P = p - i p (a+r)/b.

But careful: In complex, if we set coordinate with real axis horizontal, then y-coordinate is imaginary part. So P = p + i y_P, with y_P negative. So y_P = -p (a+r)/b. That's consistent.

Now AP direction: from A to P: v = P - A = (p - a) + i (y_P - b) = Δ - i (p (a+r)/b + b). Actually y_P = -p(a+r)/b, so y_P - b = -p(a+r)/b - b = -(p(a+r)+b^2)/b. But b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So p(a+r)+r^2 - a^2 = (a+r)(p + r - a) as before. So y_P - b = - (a+r)(p+r-a)/b = - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b. So v = Δ - i (a+r)(Δ+r)/b. So the direction vector (dx, dy) corresponds to (Δ, - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b). Good.

Now E = second intersection of line through A in direction v with circle Ω. In complex, we can find E by solving: A + t v lies on |z|=r, with t real. Since |A|^2=r^2, we get 2t Re(A conj(v)) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t=0 or t = -2 Re(A conj(v))/|v|^2. So t_E = -2 Re(A conj(v))/|v|^2. Similarly, for circle centered at d (real), we have |A + t v - d|^2 = R^2 => |A-d|^2 + 2t Re((A-d) conj(v)) + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 => since |A-d|^2=R^2, we get t = -2 Re((A-d) conj(v))/|v|^2. So t_F = -2 Re((A-d) conj(v))/|v|^2.

We need O such that |O| = |O-d| = |O-P|. Since 0 and d are real, the perpendicular bisector of 0 and d is the vertical line Re(z)=d/2. So O = d/2 + i y_O. Then condition |O|^2 = |O-d|^2 is automatically satisfied because O is equidistant from 0 and d. Now we need |O|^2 = |O-P|^2. Compute:

|O|^2 = (d/2)^2 + y_O^2.

|O-P|^2 = |(d/2 - p) + i (y_O - y_P)|^2 = (d/2 - p)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2.

Set equal:

(d/2)^2 + y_O^2 = (d/2 - p)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2 = (d/2)^2 - d p + p^2 + y_O^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2.

Cancel (d/2)^2 and y_O^2:

0 = -d p + p^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2 => 2 y_O y_P = p^2 - d p + y_P^2.

Thus y_O = (p^2 - d p + y_P^2)/(2 y_P). Note y_P is negative.

Now H = z1+z2+z3 - 2O = (P + 0 + d) - 2O = (p + i y_P + d) - 2(d/2 + i y_O) = (p+d + i y_P) - (d + 2i y_O) = p + i y_P - 2i y_O = p + i (y_P - 2y_O).

Thus H = p + i (y_P - 2y_O). So x_H = p, y_H = y_P - 2y_O.

Now compute y_O from above: y_O = (p^2 - d p + y_P^2)/(2 y_P). Then 2y_O = (p^2 - d p + y_P^2)/y_P. So y_H = y_P - (p^2 - d p + y_P^2)/y_P = (y_P^2 - (p^2 - d p + y_P^2))/y_P = (-p^2 + d p)/y_P = p(d-p)/y_P. This matches our earlier expression: y_H = p(d-p)/y_P. So H = (p, p(d-p)/y_P). Good.

So distance = | (vector from H to O) dot (7,1) | / |(7,1)|. Compute dot: (-0.5)*7 + (-1.5)*1 = -3.5 -1.5 = -5. So absolute value 5. |(7,1)| = sqrt(49+1)=√50=5√2. So distance = 5/(5√2)=1/√2 = √2/2 ≈0.7071. That equals the radius r_BEF = √0.5 = 1/√2. So line L is tangent to circle (BEF). So indeed the statement holds for this numeric example.

Better to stick with analytic.

We can attempt to compute O_BEF and radius in terms of a,b,c,r,R. Let's do that.

Set:

- M = (0,0), N = (c,0) where c = MN > 0.
- Ω: x^2+y^2 = r^2.
- Γ: (x-c)^2+y^2 = R^2.
- Intersection points: A = (a,b) with a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), b = sqrt(r^2 - a^2) > 0.
- B = (a, -b).
- C = (-r,0), D = (c+R,0). (since order C-M-N-D, C is left of M, D right of N)
- p = (c+R-r)/2. So P = (p, y_P) with y_P = -p(a+r)/b.
- v = (dx, dy) = (p - a, y_P - b) = (p - a, -p(a+r)/b - b) = (Δ, - (a+r)(p+r-a)/b) = (Δ, - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b).

We have:

Δ = p - a.

Now t_E = -2 Re(A conj(v))/|v|^2. But we can also compute E as the second intersection of line through A with direction v and circle Ω. Since A is on Ω, we can use the formula: For circle centered at O, the chord through A with direction v, the other intersection is given by A + t_E v, where t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2 (if O=0). So t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2. And A·v = x0 dx + b dy = a Δ + b dy. But we already computed A·v = -r(p+r). So t_E = -2*(-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.

Similarly, t_F = -2 ((A-c,0)·v)/|v|^2? Actually for circle centered at c (real), condition: |(A + t v) - c|^2 = R^2. Since |A-c|^2=R^2, we get t = -2 ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2. So t_F = -2 ((a-c, b)·v)/|v|^2). We computed (A-c)·v = -(c+r)Δ - r(a+r). So t_F = -2 * ( -(c+r)Δ - r(a+r) ) / |v|^2 = 2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/|v|^2.

Now, we can compute E and F coordinates. Let Q = |v|^2.

Then:

E = A + (2r(p+r)/Q) v = (a + (2r(p+r)/Q) Δ, b + (2r(p+r)/Q) dy).

F = A + (2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q) v = (a + (2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q) Δ, b + (2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q) dy).

Now, B = (a, -b).

We need circumcircle of B, E, F. Since E and F are collinear with A (they lie on line AP), and B is symmetric of A across x-axis. Perhaps the circle (BEF) has some symmetry: maybe its center lies on the line through the midpoint of AB? Actually AB is vertical line x=a. The center O_BEF might lie on some line. In the example, O_BEF = (2.5, -2.5). The line x = a = 1.8 is vertical; O_BEF is not on that. But note that O_BEF has y-coordinate -2.5, which is the negative of something? B's y-coordinate is -2.4, E's y is -1.8, F's y is -3.2. So O_BEF is (2.5, -2.5). The line through O_BEF parallel to x-axis? Not.

Maybe we can find O_BEF as the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF. That might be messy but we can attempt to compute in general.

Let’s denote:

- Let t_E = α, t_F = β, where α = 2r(p+r)/Q, β = 2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q.
- Then E = (a + α Δ, b + α dy), F = (a + β Δ, b + β dy).

Note: dy = - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b.

Also, we have relation between a, b, c, r, R: a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), b^2 = r^2 - a^2.

Also, p = (c+R-r)/2, so p+r = (c+R+r)/2, and p - a = Δ.

Now, we need to compute the circumcenter O = (x_O, y_O) of triangle BEF.

We can use formula for circumcenter given three points. But perhaps we can find O by noting that B, E, F are all related to A and the x-axis. Maybe the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter something? Or maybe O lies on the line through H and some point.

In the example, O_BEF = (2.5, -2.5). The line through H (3,-1) parallel to AP has equation we used. The distance from O to that line equals radius. Perhaps we can directly compute the distance from O to line L and show it equals radius.

Line L: through H = (p, p(c-p)/y_P) = (p, p(c-p)/y_P) (since y_P negative). But we can also express H as (p, p(c-p)/y_P). We have y_P = -p(a+r)/b, so p(c-p)/y_P = p(c-p) / ( -p(a+r)/b ) = -b(c-p)/(a+r). So H = (p, -b(c-p)/(a+r)). That is a nicer expression: y_H = -b(c-p)/(a+r).

Now, direction v = (Δ, dy) = (p-a, - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b). Note that v is proportional to (Δ, - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b). Also, we can compute a perpendicular vector to v. Since we want distance from O to line L, we can compute the line equation.

But perhaps we can find O more directly. Since B = (a, -b), E = (a + α Δ, b + α dy), F = (a + β Δ, b + β dy). Note that dy is negative, so the y-coordinates of E and F are less than b? Actually b positive, dy negative, so b + α dy could be positive or negative depending on α. In example, b=2.4, dy = - (a+r)(Δ+r)/b = - (1.8+3)*(1.2+3)/2.4 = -4.8*4.2/2.4 = -20.16/2.4 = -8.4, α = 0.5, so b+α dy = 2.4 -4.2 = -1.8 (negative). So E and F are below x-axis.

We can compute circumcenter O = (x_O, y_O). One approach: Find the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF. The midpoint of BE: M_BE = ((a + a+αΔ)/2, (-b + b+α dy)/2) = (a + (αΔ)/2, (α dy)/2). The slope of BE: ( (b+α dy) - (-b) )/( (a+αΔ) - a ) = (2b + α dy)/(αΔ). So the perpendicular slope is - (αΔ)/(2b + α dy). The equation of perpendicular bisector of BE: (y - (α dy)/2) = - (αΔ)/(2b + α dy) (x - a - (αΔ)/2). Similarly for BF. This looks messy.

Maybe we can find O by using the fact that B, E, F are on a circle, and we know the line through A and P. There might be a known configuration: The points B, E, F lie on a circle that is the reflection of something across the line of centers. Alternatively, consider the inversion with center A that swaps Ω and Γ? Let's explore synthetic approach: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have PA = PC = PD. Also, since A, C, D are points, and line MN is the line of centers, we have C and D on MN. So the other intersection of Ω with MN is (r,0) which is on the same side as N relative to M. That point is not used. But perhaps we can consider the power of M and N with respect to circles.

We have A, B are the intersection points of Ω and Γ. So AB is the common chord. Also, the radical axis of Ω and Γ is line AB. So for any point X on AB, the powers to Ω and Γ are equal. In particular, the power of A is zero to both, but that's trivial.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle ACD. Its center P. Since C and D are on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is the line through the midpoint of CD perpendicular to MN. That line is vertical (if MN horizontal). So P lies on that vertical line. Also, note that M and N are on MN, so they are symmetric with respect to that vertical line? Not exactly: The midpoint of CD is p = (C+D)/2. In our coordinates, C = -r, D = c+R, so midpoint = (c+R - r)/2. M = 0, N = c. So the vertical line through p is not necessarily the perpendicular bisector of MN. The midpoint of MN is c/2. So the vertical line through p is at x=p, which is generally different from c/2. So P lies on that vertical line.

Actually for two circles intersecting, the chord distance from centers: the x-coordinate a is the coordinate of the intersection points, and we have a^2 + b^2 = r^2, so b real if |a| ≤ r. Compute circumcenter O_PMN: Since M and N on x-axis, perpendicular bisector of MN is x=2. So O = (2, y_O). So distance ≈0.629, radius ≈0.634, close but not exactly equal due to rounding. Let's compute more precisely with exact fractions? Possibly they are equal. So in this case, O_BEF is not the circumcenter of PMN, but the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). So our analytic proof should handle general.

Thus we need to compute O_BEF and radius, and then show that the distance from O_BEF to line L equals the radius.

Given the complexity, we can try to derive formulas using complex numbers and vector algebra. Since we have param t_E and t_F, we can compute O_BEF as the circumcenter of triangle with vertices B, E, F. There might be a relation: Since E and F lie on line through A with direction v, and B is the reflection of A across x-axis, perhaps the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle through A and the reflections of something? Or maybe we can consider the circle with diameter AH? Not.

Alternatively, we can use coordinate geometry with symbolic variables but try to simplify using known relationships. Let's set up coordinate system as before: M=(0,0), N=(c,0), C=(-r,0), D=(c+R,0). A=(a,b) with a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), b^2 = r^2 - a^2. Let’s also define:

Let’s denote:

- p = (c+R-r)/2.
- Δ = p - a.
- d = c (to avoid confusion with point D). I'll use d for MN length; but we already used c for d. I'll use d = MN (real). So d = c.

Now, we have:

- y_P = -p(a+r)/b.

We also have:

- v = (Δ, y_P - b) = (Δ, -p(a+r)/b - b) = (Δ, -(a+r)(p+r-a)/b) = (Δ, -(a+r)(Δ+r)/b). So let’s define:

Let’s denote:

- u = a+r (positive).
- Then v = (Δ, -u(Δ+r)/b). (Note: Δ = p - a, so Δ+r = p - a + r = p + (r - a) = p + (r - a). But we can keep.)

Now, Q = |v|^2 = Δ^2 + u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

Let’s also compute t_E = 2r(p+r)/Q. And t_F = 2((c+r)Δ + r(a+r))/Q = 2((c+r)Δ + r u)/Q.

Now, E = (a + t_E Δ, b + t_E dy), where dy = -u(Δ+r)/b.
So E = (a + α Δ, b - α u (Δ+r)/b) where α = t_E.

Similarly, F = (a + β Δ, b - β u (Δ+r)/b) where β = t_F.

B = (a, -b).

Now, we need circumcenter O = (x_O, y_O) of B, E, F.

Observe that B, E, F are all points of the form (a + k Δ, -b + something? Actually E and F have y-coordinate b - α u (Δ+r)/b, and B has y = -b. So the y-coordinates are not symmetric.

Perhaps we can find O by solving the system that O is equidistant to B and E, and to B and F. That gives:

(x_O - a)^2 + (y_O + b)^2 = (x_O - a - αΔ)^2 + (y_O + b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2? Wait: For B: (x_O - a)^2 + (y_O + b)^2. For E: (x_O - (a+αΔ))^2 + (y_O - (b - α u (Δ+r)/b))^2 = (x_O - a - αΔ)^2 + (y_O - b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2. Equate:

(x_O - a)^2 + (y_O + b)^2 = (x_O - a - αΔ)^2 + (y_O - b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2.

Expand both sides. Let’s denote X = x_O - a, Y = y_O + b. Then left = X^2 + Y^2.

Right: (X - αΔ)^2 + ( (y_O - b) + α u (Δ+r)/b )^2 = (X - αΔ)^2 + ( (Y - 2b) + α u (Δ+r)/b )^2? Since y_O = Y - b? Actually Y = y_O + b => y_O = Y - b. Then y_O - b = Y - 2b. So right = (X - αΔ)^2 + (Y - 2b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2.

Thus equation:

X^2 + Y^2 = (X - αΔ)^2 + (Y - 2b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2.

Expand:

X^2 + Y^2 = X^2 - 2αΔ X + α^2 Δ^2 + (Y - 2b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2.

Cancel X^2:

Y^2 = -2αΔ X + α^2 Δ^2 + (Y - 2b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2.

Now expand (Y - 2b + α u (Δ+r)/b)^2 = (Y - 2b)^2 + 2(Y-2b) α u (Δ+r)/b + α^2 u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

So:

Y^2 = -2αΔ X + α^2 Δ^2 + (Y-2b)^2 + 2α u (Δ+r)(Y-2b)/b + α^2 u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

Now, (Y-2b)^2 = Y^2 -4bY +4b^2. So:

Y^2 = -2αΔ X + α^2 Δ^2 + Y^2 -4bY +4b^2 + 2α u (Δ+r)(Y-2b)/b + α^2 u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

Cancel Y^2 on both sides:

0 = -2αΔ X + α^2 Δ^2 -4bY +4b^2 + 2α u (Δ+r)(Y-2b)/b + α^2 u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

Thus:

2αΔ X + 4bY = α^2 Δ^2 +4b^2 + 2α u (Δ+r)(Y-2b)/b + α^2 u^2 (Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

But note: Y = y_O + b, and X = x_O - a. So this is one equation linking x_O and y_O.

Similarly, from B and F, we get another equation with β instead of α. That might be messy.

Maybe we can find O more cleverly: Since B is the reflection of A across the x-axis, and E and F are on line through A with direction v, perhaps the circle (BEF) is the reflection of the circle through A and the points where the line meets something? Or maybe we can use the concept of Miquel point: Given triangle ACD and points B? Not.

Another approach: Use inversion about the circle with center A that maps Ω to a line? Since A is on Ω and Γ, an inversion with center A and arbitrary radius will send Ω to a line (since A lies on Ω, the inversion maps Ω to a line not passing through A). Similarly, Γ maps to another line. The points C and D, being on Ω and Γ, will map to points C' and D' on those lines. The circumcenter P of ACD, under inversion, will map to something? Inversion does not preserve circumcenters directly, but it sends circles through A to lines, and points on the circle map to points on the line. Also, the line AP (through A) maps to itself (since it passes through A). But under inversion, the line through A is invariant as a set (though points map along it). So E and F, being the second intersections of AP with Ω and Γ, will map to points E' and F' on the lines (images of Ω and Γ). In fact, since A is on both circles, the inversion will map each circle to a line; the point A goes to infinity on that line? Actually careful: Inversion with center A: any circle passing through A becomes a line not passing through A. So Ω (passing through A) maps to a line L_Ω. Similarly, Γ maps to a line L_Γ. The points E and F are the second intersections of AP with Ω and Γ. Given the complexity, perhaps we can use coordinates and compute O_BEF and radius in terms of a,b,c,r,R, then show that the distance from O_BEF to line L equals the radius. That is a straightforward algebraic verification, albeit messy. But with careful symbolic manipulation, we can simplify using the relations among a,b,c,r,R. Since a and b are expressed in terms of c,r,R, we can eliminate a and b in favor of c,r,R. Also p = (c+R-r)/2, Δ = p - a, u = a+r. So everything is in terms of c,r,R. Then we can compute O_BEF and radius, and then compute the distance from O_BEF to line L (which also depends on p, y_H, and direction v). The goal is to show that distance equals radius.

This is a heavy algebraic task but manageable with systematic simplification. Since we are doing it manually, we need to be careful. We can use vector methods and perhaps some clever factorizations.

Let's define:

- c = MN (positive real).
- r = radius of Ω, R = radius of Γ, with r < R.
- a = (c^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2c). So a is real.
- b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So b = sqrt(r^2 - a^2) > 0.
- u = a + r > 0.
- p = (c + R - r)/2.
- Δ = p - a.

We also have y_P = -p u / b.
- v = (Δ, -u(Δ+r)/b). Note: Δ+r = p - a + r = p + (r - a). Since a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), r-a = r - (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c) = (2cr - c^2 - r^2 + R^2)/(2c) = (R^2 - (c - r)^2)/(2c) = (R^2 - (c - r)^2)/(2c). This is positive because circles intersect and r<R? Actually need to ensure that a < r? For intersection, the distance from M to chord AB is a, and we have |a| ≤ r. Since r<R, a could be negative or positive. But we are using a as coordinate; we have r-a =? Not necessarily positive. However, u = a+r > 0 always. Δ+r = p + (r - a). Since p = (c+R-r)/2, Δ+r = (c+R-r)/2 + (r - a) = (c+R-r+2r-2a)/2 = (c+R+r-2a)/2. That might be positive. We'll keep.

Now, Q = |v|^2 = Δ^2 + u^2(Δ+r)^2 / b^2.

Let’s also define:

- α = 2r(p+r)/Q.
- β = 2((c+r)Δ + r u)/Q.

Note that p+r = (c+R-r)/2 + r = (c+R+r)/2. So p+r = (c+R+r)/2.

Also, (c+r)Δ + r u = (c+r)(p-a) + r(a+r) = (c+r)p - (c+r)a + r a + r^2 = (c+r)p - c a - r a + r a + r^2 = (c+r)p - c a + r^2.

But maybe we can find a simpler expression for β. Since we have relations, maybe β simplifies nicely. Let's compute β in terms of known.

We have β = 2((c+r)Δ + r u)/Q. But also note that Δ = p - a, and u = a+r. So:

(c+r)Δ + r u = (c+r)(p-a) + r(a+r) = (c+r)p - (c+r)a + r a + r^2 = (c+r)p - c a + r^2.

Now, we can also compute (c+r)p = (c+r)(c+R-r)/2 = ( (c+r)(c+R-r) )/2. And c a = c * (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c) = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/2. So:

(c+r)Δ + r u = ((c+r)(c+R-r) - (c^2+r^2-R^2) + 2r^2)/2? Actually careful: (c+r)p - c a + r^2 = (c+r)(c+R-r)/2 - (c^2+r^2-R^2)/2 + r^2 = (1/2)[(c+r)(c+R-r) - (c^2+r^2-R^2) + 2r^2] = (1/2)[ (c+r)(c+R-r) - c^2 - r^2 + R^2 + 2r^2] = (1/2)[ (c+r)(c+R-r) - c^2 + r^2 + R^2].

Now expand (c+r)(c+R-r) = c(c+R-r) + r(c+R-r) = c^2 + cR - cr + rc + rR - r^2 = c^2 + cR + rR - r^2. So:

(c+r)(c+R-r) - c^2 + r^2 + R^2 = (c^2 + cR + rR - r^2) - c^2 + r^2 + R^2 = cR + rR + R^2 = R(c + r + R). So:

(c+r)Δ + r u = (1/2) * R(c+r+R) = R(c+r+R)/2.

Thus β = 2 * (R(c+r+R)/2) / Q = R(c+r+R)/Q.

Nice! So β = R(c+r+R)/Q. And α = 2r(p+r)/Q = 2r * ((c+R+r)/2)/Q = r(c+R+r)/Q.

So α = r(c+R+r)/Q, β = R(c+r+R)/Q.

Thus t_E = α = r(c+R+r)/Q, t_F = β = R(c+r+R)/Q. That is much simpler.

E = A + α v = (a + α Δ, b + α dy).

Now, we also have B = (a, -b). And we want circumcenter O of B, E, F. Since E and F are on line through A with direction v, we can consider the circle through B and the two points where a line through A meets the circles. Perhaps we can find O as the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of BE and the perpendicular bisector of BF. Let's compute the perpendicular bisector of BE.

Let’s denote:

- Let’s define parameter t (real) such that point = A + t v. Then for t = α we get E, for t = β we get F, and for t = 0 we get A. Also, B = (a, -b) = (a, -b) = (a, b) - (0, 2b)? Actually A = (a,b). So B = A - (0,2b). So B = A - 2b j, where j is unit vector in y-direction.

Now, we can compute the midpoint of BE: M_BE = ( (a + (a+αΔ))/2, (-b + (b+α dy))/2 ) = (a + (αΔ)/2, (α dy)/2). Similarly, the vector from B to E: E - B = (αΔ, b+α dy + b) = (αΔ, 2b + α dy). So the perpendicular bisector of BE is the set of points X such that (X - M_BE) dot (E - B) = 0 (since perpendicular to BE). That is: (x - a - αΔ/2) * (αΔ) + (y - α dy/2) * (2b + α dy) = 0.

Similarly, for BF: M_BF = (a + βΔ/2, β dy/2). Vector B to F: (βΔ, 2b + β dy). Equation: (x - a - βΔ/2) * (βΔ) + (y - β dy/2) * (2b + β dy) = 0.

We need to solve these two equations to find X = (x_O, y_O). This might simplify because the equations are linear in x and y. Let's denote:

Equation (1): αΔ x + (2b+α dy) y = αΔ (a + αΔ/2) + (2b+α dy) (α dy/2) = αΔ a + α^2 Δ^2/2 + (2b+α dy) α dy/2.

Equation (2): βΔ x + (2b+β dy) y = βΔ (a + βΔ/2) + (2b+β dy) (β dy/2) = βΔ a + β^2 Δ^2/2 + (2b+β dy) β dy/2.

We can write these as:

αΔ x + (2b+α dy) y = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b+α dy) α dy/2.   (1)
βΔ x + (2b+β dy) y = βΔ a + (β^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b+β dy) β dy/2.   (2)

We can solve for x and y by treating as linear system in x and y. Since the coefficients are not symmetric, but we can subtract multiples to eliminate x or y. Let's do:

Let’s denote:

A1 = αΔ, B1 = 2b+α dy, C1 = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b+α dy) α dy/2.
A2 = βΔ, B2 = 2b+β dy, C2 = βΔ a + (β^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b+β dy) β dy/2.

We want to solve:

A1 x + B1 y = C1
A2 x + B2 y = C2

We can solve by Cramer's rule. The determinant D = A1 B2 - A2 B1 = αΔ (2b+β dy) - βΔ (2b+α dy) = Δ [ α(2b+β dy) - β(2b+α dy) ] = Δ [ 2bα + αβ dy - 2bβ - αβ dy ] = Δ [ 2b(α - β) ] = 2b Δ (α - β). So D = 2b Δ (α - β).

Now, α and β: α = r(c+R+r)/Q, β = R(c+r+R)/Q. So α - β = (r(c+R+r) - R(c+r+R))/Q = (r(c+R+r) - R(c+r+R))/Q. Expand: r(c+R+r) = r c + r R + r^2. R(c+r+R) = R c + R r + R^2. So difference = (r c + r R + r^2) - (R c + R r + R^2) = r c + r R + r^2 - R c - R r - R^2 = (r c - R c) + (r R - R r) + (r^2 - R^2) = c(r - R) + 0 + (r - R)(r+R) = (r - R)(c + r + R) = -(R - r)(c + r + R). So α - β = -(R - r)(c + r + R)/Q. Thus α - β is negative (since R>r). So D = 2b Δ (α - β) = -2b Δ (R - r)(c + r + R)/Q. But note that Δ = p - a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. Also, we have b>0, so D is nonzero if Δ≠0. Could Δ be zero? That would mean p = a, i.e., (c+R-r)/2 = a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Multiply by 2c: c(c+R-r) = c^2+r^2-R^2 => c^2 + cR - cr = c^2+r^2-R^2 => cR - cr = r^2 - R^2 => R(c - r) = -(R^2 - r^2) = -(R-r)(R+r) => If R>r, divide by (R-r): R = -(R+r) => 2R + r = 0, impossible. So Δ ≠ 0. So D ≠ 0.

Now, x_O = (C1 B2 - C2 B1)/D. y_O = (A1 C2 - A2 C1)/D.

We need to compute these. Let's compute C1 and C2.

First, simplify C1:

C1 = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b+α dy) α dy/2 = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b α dy)/2 + (α^2 dy^2)/2 = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + b α dy + (α^2 dy^2)/2.

Similarly, C2 = βΔ a + (β^2 Δ^2)/2 + b β dy + (β^2 dy^2)/2.

Now, we can also compute the numerator for x_O:

Num_x = C1 B2 - C2 B1 = C1 (2b+β dy) - C2 (2b+α dy).

Since B2 = 2b+β dy, B1 = 2b+α dy.

Now, note that C1 and C2 have terms that are linear in α and quadratic in α. We can try to factor something.

Thus C1 = α(Δ a + b dy) + (α^2/2) Q.

Similarly, C2 = β(Δ a + b dy) + (β^2/2) Q? Actually check: C2 = βΔ a + b β dy + (β^2/2)(Δ^2+dy^2) = β(Δ a + b dy) + (β^2/2) Q. But is Δ a + b dy correct? In C2, we have term b β dy, so yes, C2 = β(Δ a + b dy) + (β^2/2) Q.

Now, we need Δ a + b dy. Compute dy = -u(Δ+r)/b. So b dy = -u(Δ+r). Thus Δ a + b dy = Δ a - u(Δ+r). But u = a+r. So:

C2 = β * (-r(p+r)) + (β^2/2) Q = -β r(p+r) + (β^2/2) Q.

Maybe we can use the concept of the "pedal circle" or "orthocentric line". Another approach: Use coordinate geometry but with a more clever choice of coordinates that simplifies the algebra. Then the circles are defined by distances to centers: For Ω: t^2 + h^2 = r^2, and for Γ: (t-d)^2 + h^2 = R^2. So we have r^2 = t^2 + h^2, R^2 = (t-d)^2 + h^2. PC^2 = (p + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Set equal: (p-t)^2 + (y_P - h)^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2 => (p-t)^2 + y_P^2 -2h y_P + h^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2 => (p-t)^2 -2h y_P + h^2 = (p+r)^2 => -2h y_P = (p+r)^2 - (p-t)^2 - h^2 = ( (p+r)^2 - (p-t)^2 ) - h^2 = ( (p+r - (p-t)) (p+r + (p-t)) ) - h^2 = ( (r+t)(2p + r - t) ) - h^2. Given the time constraints, I think a rigorous analytic proof is feasible if we do the algebra systematically. Given the heavy algebraic simplification we already did, perhaps we can find that O is exactly the point such that O, H, and something are related. Maybe we can compute det using the expression det = (R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) ( -2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) ) - p dy + y_H Δ. Let's plug in the known expressions for Δ, u, p, dy, y_H in terms of c,R and b. Since b appears, we need to express b in terms of c,R. We already have b = sqrt( (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2) )/(2c). That is sqrt of product. Let's compute the expression for det using the derived simplified Q and other constants. We have:

det = (R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) * ( -2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) ) + p u (Δ+1)/b - b Δ (c-p)/u.

Now, we can compute each term in terms of c,R and b. But b appears in denominators; however, we can factor something like (c+R+1)/(4bΔ) times something. Let's compute -2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1). We have:

u(Δ+1) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(4c^2). So -2u(Δ+1) = - (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c^2). Then plus (c+R+1). So:

-2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) = (c+R+1) - (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c^2).

Now, note that (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. And (c+R-1) =? Also, we have b^2 = (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2). So (R^2 - (c-1)^2) = (R-(c-1))(R+(c-1)) = (R-c+1)(R+c-1). And ((c+1)^2 - R^2) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R). So b^2 = (R-c+1)(R+c-1)(c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2). So (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = b^2 * 4c^2 / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). Then the term (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c^2) = [b^2 * 4c^2 / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1))] * (R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c^2) = b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). So:

-2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) = (c+R+1) - b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)).

Now, we also have Δ = (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c). So (c+R+1)/(4bΔ) = (c+R+1) / (4b * (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c)) = (c+R+1) * (2c) / (4b (R-1)(c+R+1)) = (2c) / (4b (R-1)) = c/(2b(R-1)). So:

(R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) * ( -2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) ) = (c/(2b(R-1))) * ( (c+R+1) - b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) )? Wait careful: The factor is (R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) times the expression. Since we have (R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) = (c/(2b(R-1))) as derived. So:

Term1 = (c/(2b(R-1))) * ( (c+R+1) - b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) ).

Now, term2: p u (Δ+1)/b. We have p = (c+R-1)/2. u = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c). Δ+1 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c). So u(Δ+1) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c) * (R+1)(c+R-1)/(2c) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(4c^2). Then p u (Δ+1) = (c+R-1)/2 * (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(4c^2) = (c+R-1)^2 (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)/(8c^2). Then p u (Δ+1)/b = (c+R-1)^2 (c+1-R)(c+1+R)(R+1)/(8c^2 b). But note that (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. And we also have b^2 = (R-c+1)(R+c-1)(c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2) = (R-c+1)(R+c-1)((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2). So (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = b^2 * 4c^2 / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). So we can substitute:

p u (Δ+1)/b = (c+R-1)^2 * [b^2 * 4c^2 / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1))] * (R+1)/(8c^2 b) = (c+R-1)^2 * b^2 * 4c^2 * (R+1) / (8c^2 b (R-c+1)(R+c-1)) = (c+R-1)^2 * b * (R+1) / (2 (R-c+1)(R+c-1)).

Thus term2 = (c+R-1)^2 * b * (R+1) / (2 (R-c+1)(R+c-1)).

Now, term3: - b Δ (c-p)/u. We have Δ = (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c). c-p = c - (c+R-1)/2 = (c - R +1)/2. u = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c). So:

b Δ (c-p)/u = b * [(R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c)] * [(c - R +1)/2] / [ (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c) ] = b * (R-1)(c+R+1)(c - R +1) / (4c) * (2c) / ((c+1-R)(c+1+R)) = b * (R-1)(c+R+1)(c - R +1) / (2) * 1 / ((c+1-R)(c+1+R)). Now, note that (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. Also, (c+R+1)(c - R +1) = (c+1)^2 - R^2 = same! Because (c+1+R)(c+1-R) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. So indeed, (c+R+1)(c - R +1) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. So:

b Δ (c-p)/u = b * (R-1) * ((c+1)^2 - R^2) / (2) * 1 / ((c+1)^2 - R^2) = b * (R-1)/2. So term3 = - b * (R-1)/2.

Thus det = Term1 + Term2 + Term3 = [c/(2b(R-1)) * ( (c+R+1) - b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) )] + [ (c+R-1)^2 * b * (R+1) / (2 (R-c+1)(R+c-1)) ] - b (R-1)/2.

Now, we need to simplify this. It seems we have terms with b in denominator and numerator. Let's combine.

Let’s denote:

A = (c+R+1) - b^2 * 2 (R+1)(c+R-1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). Then Term1 = c/(2b(R-1)) * A.

Term2 = (c+R-1)^2 * b * (R+1) / (2 (R-c+1)(R+c-1)).
Term3 = - b (R-1)/2.

We can try to write everything over a common denominator. Let's compute Term2 - Term3: = b [ (c+R-1)^2 (R+1) / (2 (R-c+1)(R+c-1)) - (R-1)/2 ] = b/2 [ (c+R-1)^2 (R+1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) - (R-1) ].

Now, Term1 = c/(2b(R-1)) * A. So det = cA/(2b(R-1)) + b/2 [ (c+R-1)^2 (R+1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) - (R-1) ].

Now, we need to express A in terms of c,R,b. A = (c+R+1) - 2b^2 (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). So cA/(2b(R-1)) = c/(2b(R-1)) * (c+R+1) - c/(2b(R-1)) * 2b^2 (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) - c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R-c+1)(R+c-1)). Because 2b^2 * c/(2b(R-1)) = c b/(R-1). So:

Term1 = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) - c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R-c+1)(R+c-1)).

Thus det = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) - c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R-c+1)(R+c-1)) + b/2 [ (c+R-1)^2 (R+1) / ((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) - (R-1) ].

Now, combine the terms with b in denominator and numerator. Notice that the second term and the third term have b in numerator. The first term has b in denominator. So to combine, we might need to find a relation between b and c,R. Since b = sqrt( (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2) )/(2c). So b^2 = (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2). So b^2 is expressed as product. This might allow simplification.

Let's compute the product (R-c+1)(R+c-1). That is (R-(c-1))(R+(c-1)) = R^2 - (c-1)^2. So that factor appears in b^2. Also, ((c+1)^2 - R^2) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R). So b^2 = (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2). So we have:

(R^2 - (c-1)^2) = (R-c+1)(R+c-1). And ((c+1)^2 - R^2) = (c+1-R)(c+1+R). So:

b^2 = (R-c+1)(R+c-1)(c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2).

Thus, we can write:

c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R-c+1)(R+c-1)) = c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R-c+1)(R+c-1)). Note that (R-c+1)(R+c-1) = (R^2 - (c-1)^2). So this term is c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-1)(R^2 - (c-1)^2)). And we also have the third term: b/2 * (c+R-1)^2 (R+1)/((R-c+1)(R+c-1)) = b/2 * (c+R-1)^2 (R+1)/(R^2 - (c-1)^2). So both terms have denominator R^2 - (c-1)^2. So we can combine them.

Let’s define:

D = R^2 - (c-1)^2 = (R-c+1)(R+c-1). Then:

Term2 (the - c b ...) = - c b (R+1)(c+R-1)/[(R-1) D].

Term3 (the b/2 ...) = b/2 * (c+R-1)^2 (R+1)/D.

So their sum = b (R+1)/D * [ - c (c+R-1)/(R-1) + (c+R-1)^2/2 ].

Simplify inside brackets: = (c+R-1) [ - c/(R-1) + (c+R-1)/2 ].

Thus sum = b (R+1)(c+R-1)/D * [ - c/(R-1) + (c+R-1)/2 ].

Now, also we have the first term: c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)). So det = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) + b (R+1)(c+R-1)/D * [ - c/(R-1) + (c+R-1)/2 ].

Now, we need to show that det^2 = |O-B|^2 * Q. But maybe we can compute det in a simpler form by using the relation between b and D. Since b^2 = D * (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2). But (c+1-R)(c+1+R) = (c+1)^2 - R^2. So we can write b = sqrt( D * ((c+1)^2 - R^2) )/(2c). That still has sqrt.

Maybe we can compute det as a rational function of c and R after squaring? But perhaps there is a simpler expression for det. Let's compute the bracket:

- c/(R-1) + (c+R-1)/2 = [ -2c + (R-1)(c+R-1) ] / [2(R-1)] = [ -2c + (R-1)(c+R-1) ] / [2(R-1)].

Compute (R-1)(c+R-1) = (R-1)c + (R-1)(R-1) = cR - c + R^2 -2R +1. So -2c + that = cR - c -2c + R^2 -2R +1 = cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1. So bracket = (cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1) / [2(R-1)].

Thus sum = b (R+1)(c+R-1)/D * (cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1) / [2(R-1)].

So det = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) + b (R+1)(c+R-1)(cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1) / [2D (R-1)].

Now, note that D = R^2 - (c-1)^2 = R^2 - (c^2 -2c +1) = R^2 - c^2 + 2c -1. So D = (R^2 - c^2) + 2c -1.

Also, c(c+R+1) = c^2 + cR + c.

We still have b in first term. To combine, we could write det as something like (c^2 + cR + c)/(2b(R-1)) + something. This is messy.

Given the extreme algebraic complexity, I suspect there is a synthetic solution that is much more elegant. Let's try to think again.

We have triangle ACD and its circumcenter P. The line AP is the line through A and the circumcenter of ACD. There is a known fact: The line joining a vertex to the circumcenter is the perpendicular bisector of the opposite side? Not. But there is a relation: If we reflect A across the perpendicular bisector of CD, we get some point on the circle. Since P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, reflecting A across that line gives a point A' such that PA = PA' and A' is symmetric. That might be related to something.

Alternatively, consider the circle with diameter PM? Not.

Another idea: Use the fact that H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is the point such that PH ⟂ MN, MH ⟂ PN, NH ⟂ PM. Since MN is horizontal, PH is vertical, so H has same x-coordinate as P. So H = (p, y_H). And we have that the line through H parallel to AP is L. So L has direction v = P-A. So L is the line through H with direction v.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. We need to show that L is tangent to it. That is equivalent to saying that the distance from the center O of that circle to L equals the radius. Perhaps we can find O as the midpoint of the segment connecting H and something? In the numeric example, O = (0.8333,-0.8333), H = (1,-0.3333). The vector from H to O is (-0.1667,-0.5). The direction v = (0.4,-2.8). The cross product is 0.6667. The radius is distance from O to B = sqrt((0.8333-0.6)^2+(-0.8333+0.8)^2)= sqrt(0.2333^2+(-0.0333)^2)=0.2357. And |O-H| = sqrt(0.1667^2+0.5^2)=0.518. Not equal.

Maybe we can consider the reflection of H across the line of centers? In the example, reflection of H across x-axis is (1,0.3333). That is not O.

Given the difficulty, I think a synthetic solution using complex numbers with appropriate transformations might be simpler. Let's try to use complex numbers with M=0, N=c real, and set the circles as |z|=r, |z-c|=R. We have A = a+ib, B = a-ib. C = -r, D = c+R. P = circumcenter of A, C, D. Since C and D are real, the circumcenter lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is vertical line at x = (C+D)/2 = p. So P = p + i y_P, with y_P = - (a+1) p / b? Wait with r=1 scaling, we had y_P = -p(a+1)/b. But in general, with r, we have y_P = -p (a+r)/b. Because u = a+r. So P = p + i y_P.

Now, we need to find H = orthocenter of triangle P, M, N. M=0, N=c. The orthocenter H = P + M + N - 2O where O is circumcenter of PMN. Since M and N are on real axis, the circumcenter O of triangle PMN lies on perpendicular bisector of MN, which is vertical line x = c/2. So O = c/2 + i y_O. Then H = P + 0 + c - 2O = (p + c) + i y_P - 2(c/2 + i y_O) = p + i y_P - 2i y_O = p + i(y_P - 2y_O). And y_O is determined by |O| = |O-P|. Compute: |O|^2 = (c/2)^2 + y_O^2. |O-P|^2 = (c/2 - p)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2. Set equal: (c/2)^2 + y_O^2 = (c/2 - p)^2 + y_O^2 - 2y_O y_P + y_P^2 => cancel y_O^2: (c/2)^2 = (c/2 - p)^2 - 2y_O y_P + y_P^2 => 2y_O y_P = (c/2 - p)^2 + y_P^2 - (c/2)^2 = (c^2/4 - cp + p^2) + y_P^2 - c^2/4 = -cp + p^2 + y_P^2. Thus y_O = (p^2 + y_P^2 - cp)/(2y_P). Then y_H = y_P - 2y_O = y_P - (p^2 + y_P^2 - cp)/y_P = (y_P^2 - p^2 - y_P^2 + cp)/y_P = (cp - p^2)/y_P = p(c-p)/y_P. So H = p + i p(c-p)/y_P. Since y_P negative, H is below x-axis.

Now, we also have E = second intersection of line AP with Ω: |z|=r. Since A is on Ω, we have E = A + α v, with α = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2? Actually we derived α = 2r(p+r)/Q, where Q = |v|^2. And v = P-A. So E = A + α v. Similarly, F = A + β v, with β = 2R(c+r+R)/Q? Actually we derived β = R(c+r+R)/Q with r=1? Let's derive general: For circle centered at c (real), condition |z-c|=R. Since A satisfies, and we have line through A with direction v, the other intersection F satisfies A + t v, with t = -2 Re((A-c) conj(v))/|v|^2. And we found that (A-c)·v = - (c+r)Δ - r(a+r)? Actually we can compute in general using the fact that P is circumcenter of ACD. There might be a nice relation: Since P is the circumcenter, we have PA = PC = PD. And C and D are on real axis. So we can find expressions for PC^2 and PD^2. Then we can compute power of P with respect to Ω and Γ.

We have PA^2 = PC^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2.
Also, PM^2 = p^2 + y_P^2. So PM^2 - r^2 = p^2 + y_P^2 - r^2 = (p^2 + y_P^2 - r^2). But we also have that PA^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2 = p^2 + 2pr + r^2 + y_P^2. So PA^2 = PM^2 + 2pr + r^2. Thus PA^2 - PM^2 = 2pr + r^2. Not helpful.

We can compute PE = (PM^2 - r^2)/PA. So PE = (p^2 + y_P^2 - r^2)/PA. Similarly, PF = (PN^2 - R^2)/PA. PN^2 = (p-c)^2 + y_P^2. So PF = ((p-c)^2 + y_P^2 - R^2)/PA.

Now, the circle (BEF). Since B = conj(A) (reflection across real axis), and E and F are on line AP. Maybe we can compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF using complex numbers: For three points z1, z2, z3, the circumcenter is given by:

O = ( |z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2) ) / ( z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) )? Actually, the formula for the circumcenter in complex plane: O = ( (|z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2) ) ) / ( z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) ). But this formula gives the circumcenter if we treat points as complex numbers. However, it's often used for circumcircle passing through origin? Not sure.

But we can also use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle through A and the two points where the line through A meets the circles? Not.

Given the time, I think it's better to try to compute the condition for tangency using vectors and the expressions we have, but we need to show that the distance from O to L equals the radius. Perhaps we can compute the distance from O to L using the formula for distance from a point to a line: |det(O-H, v)|/|v|. And we need to show that this equals |O-B|. So we need to show:

|det(O-H, v)| = |O-B| * |v|.

But we have expressions for O, H, v, B in terms of coordinates. Since we already have a simplified expression for det = det(O-H, v) (which is the scalar cross product). And we have that |v| = sqrt(Q). So we need to show that det^2 = |O-B|^2 * Q.

We have det expressed in terms of c,R,b, etc. And we have O-B = (x_O - a, y_O + b). We could compute both sides using the known relations and see if they are equal. This is a polynomial identity after squaring, and we can attempt to verify it by substituting the expressions for a,b in terms of c,R. Since we have already simplified det to an expression involving b and c,R, we can also compute |O-B|^2. Maybe we can find that |O-B|^2 = (something)^2 / Q? Let's attempt.

From earlier, we have O = t*(-B1, αΔ) with t = C2/(2bΔ(α-β))? Actually we had O = t*(-B1, αΔ) with t = C2/(2bΔ(α-β)). And we derived that t = -R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ) for r=1. Then O = (R(c+R+1)B1/(4bΔ), -Rα(c+R+1)/(4b)). And we also have B = (a, -b). So O-B = (R(c+R+1)B1/(4bΔ) - a, -Rα(c+R+1)/(4b) + b). We can compute |O-B|^2. But perhaps we can also compute det(O-H, v). Since we have det = (R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)) ( -2u(Δ+1) + (c+R+1) ) - p dy + y_H Δ. And we already simplified det to an expression. Maybe we can compute det and |O-B|^2 using the known relationships, and then show equality.

Given the complexity, but we have already derived that det = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) + b (R+1)(c+R-1)(cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1) / [2D (R-1)] with D = R^2 - (c-1)^2. We can also compute |O-B|^2. Let's compute O-B explicitly using the expressions we have for O and B. Since we have O = (x_O, y_O) and B = (a, -b). And we have expressions for a, b, p, etc. In the r=1 scaling, we have:

Δ = (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c).
u = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c) = (c+1)^2 - R^2)/(2c).
p = (c+R-1)/2.
b = sqrt( (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2) )/(2c).
α = (c+R+1)/Q, where Q = Δ^2 + u^2(Δ+1)^2/b^2. But we might not need α explicitly. We have O = (R(c+R+1)B1/(4bΔ), -R(c+R+1)^2/(4bQ)). And B1 = 2b - α u (Δ+1)/b. That's messy.

Maybe we can find a simpler representation for O. Since O is circumcenter of BEF, and B is symmetric of A, and E,F on line AP, perhaps O is the midpoint of the segment joining the orthocenter of triangle ACD and something? Not.

Another approach: Use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the circle through B and the two points where the line through A meets the circles. There is a known property: The power of the orthocenter of triangle PMN with respect to this circle equals something like (distance from H to line AP)^2? Not.

Given the difficulty, I think a coordinate approach with specific parameters might be the only way to ensure rigor. Since we have already derived many simplifications, we can attempt to compute det^2 and |O-B|^2 * Q in terms of c and R using the expressions for b^2, etc., and show they are equal. This is a heavy but doable algebraic verification. Let's try to do it systematically.

We have:

Let’s define:

- c = MN (positive real)
- R > 1
- Let’s denote:

a = (c^2+1-R^2)/(2c)
b^2 = 1 - a^2 = (R^2 - (c-1)^2)((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2)

Define:

Δ = (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c)
p = (c+R-1)/2
u = (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(2c) = ( (c+1)^2 - R^2 )/(2c)
y_P = -p u / b? Actually we have y_P = -p(a+1)/b? Wait with r=1, we had u = a+1, so y_P = -p u / b. And b is positive. So y_P = -p u / b.

Now, Q = |v|^2 = Δ^2 + u^2(Δ+1)^2 / b^2. But we already computed that u(Δ+1)/b^2 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-c+1)(R+c-1))? Actually we derived that u(Δ+1)/b^2 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/((R-c+1)(R+c-1)). Let's denote:

S = (R-c+1)(R+c-1) = (R^2 - (c-1)^2). So u(Δ+1)/b^2 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/S. Then:

Q = Δ^2 + u^2 (Δ+1)^2 / b^2 = Δ^2 + u * (u(Δ+1)/b^2) = Δ^2 + u * (R+1)(c+R-1)/S.

Now, we also have α = (c+R+1)/Q, β = R(c+R+1)/Q. So α Q = c+R+1, β Q = R(c+R+1). Good.

Now, we need O = circumcenter of B, E, F. We can compute O using the formula for circumcenter of three points. Since we have param expressions for E and F, we can compute the circle (BEF). But perhaps we can find O directly as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors, and we already have a nice expression: O lies on the line through M (origin) with direction (-B1, αΔ) where B1 = 2b - α u (Δ+1)/b? Actually we derived O = t*(-B1, αΔ) with t = C2/(2bΔ(α-β)) and then simplified t to -R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ) when r=1. So O = ( -t B1, t αΔ ). Then:

x_O = -t B1 = R(c+R+1)B1/(4bΔ) (since t = -R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ) => -t = R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ)). So x_O = R(c+R+1)B1/(4bΔ).
y_O = t αΔ = -R(c+R+1)αΔ/(4bΔ) = -R(c+R+1)α/(4b).

Now, B1 = 2b - α u (Δ+1)/b. So:

x_O = R(c+R+1) [2b - α u (Δ+1)/b] / (4bΔ) = R(c+R+1) [2b/(4bΔ) - α u (Δ+1)/(4b^2 Δ)] = R(c+R+1) [1/(2Δ) - α u (Δ+1)/(4b^2 Δ)].

Thus x_O = R(c+R+1) [1/(2Δ) - α u (Δ+1)/(4b^2 Δ)] = R(c+R+1)/(2Δ) * [1 - α u (Δ+1)/(2b^2)].

But α = (c+R+1)/Q, so α u (Δ+1)/(2b^2) = (c+R+1) u (Δ+1)/(2b^2 Q). And we have u(Δ+1)/b^2 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/S, so this term = (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2S Q). So x_O = R(c+R+1)/(2Δ) * [1 - (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2S Q)]. That seems messy.

Maybe we can compute O directly using the formula for circumcenter of triangle with vertices B, E, F. Since E and F are on line AP, and B is symmetric of A, maybe there is a known fact: The circumcenter of BEF is the midpoint of the arc AB of the circle through A, C, D? Not.

Given the extreme algebraic complexity, I suspect there is a synthetic solution that is more elegant. Let's try to think differently.

Maybe we can use properties of the Simson line. For triangle PMN, the orthocenter H. The line through H parallel to AP: since AP is a line through A and P, and H is orthocenter, perhaps this line is the reflection of something like the line through the circumcenter of triangle PMN and A? Not.

Let's attempt that: Invert about A. Choose inversion radius such that the circles become lines. Under inversion, the circles Ω and Γ become lines L_Ω and L_Γ, which intersect at B' (the image of B). The line AP passes through A, so it maps to itself (as a line through A). The second intersections E and F map to the intersections of the line AP with L_Ω and L_Γ. So E' = AP ∩ L_Ω, F' = AP ∩ L_Γ. The points C and D, being on Ω and Γ, map to points C' and D' on L_Ω and L_Γ. Actually, the circumcircle of ACD passes through A, so its image under inversion is a line not passing through A. That line is the image of the circumcircle. Since C and D map to C' and D', the line L_CD is the image of the circumcircle. And the center P of that circle does not map to the center of the line; rather, the image of P is the point that is the inverse of P. So we get that P' (inverse of P) lies on the line through C' and D'? Actually, if we invert, the center of a circle through the inversion center does not map to the center of the line; it maps to some point on the line. Now, we want O. There is a formula for circumcenter of three points z1,z2,z3:

O = ( (|z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2) ) ) / ( z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) ). This is correct for any triangle, provided the denominator is not zero. So we can compute O using this.

There is a known formula for the circumcenter of three points: O = ( |z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2) ) / ( z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) ). Let's compute numerator and denominator for z1=B, z2=E, z3=F.

Let’s denote:

z1 = B = t - i h.
z2 = A + α v = (t + i h) + α (Δ - i d) = t + αΔ + i (h - α d).
z3 = A + β v = t + βΔ + i (h - β d).

Now, compute differences:

z2 - z3 = (α - β)Δ + i ( -α d + β d) = (α - β)Δ + i d (β - α) = (α - β)(Δ - i d) = (α - β) v.

z3 - z1 = (t + βΔ + i (h - β d)) - (t - i h) = βΔ + i (h - β d + h) = βΔ + i (2h - β d).

z1 - z2 = (t - i h) - (t + αΔ + i (h - α d)) = -αΔ - i (2h - α d).

Now, compute denominator:

Denom = z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) = z1 ( (α - β) v ) + z2 (βΔ + i (2h - β d)) + z3 ( -αΔ - i (2h - α d) ).

But note that v = Δ - i d. So this is linear in α, β. Since α and β are constants, we can compute.

But perhaps we can simplify by using that α and β satisfy certain relations. We have α = (c+R+1)/Q, β = R(c+R+1)/Q. So α : β = 1 : R. So β = R α. Good! So we can set α = k, β = R k, where k = (c+R+1)/Q. That simplifies. Then:

From earlier, we have O = (x_O, y_O) with x_O = (R(c+R+1)B1)/(4bΔ), y_O = -R(c+R+1)α/(4b). And α = (c+R+1)/Q. So y_O = -R(c+R+1)^2/(4bQ). Also, B = (a, -b). So O-B = (x_O - a, y_O + b). Now, we also have the expression for det = (O-H) × v. We have H = (p, -b(c-p)/u) with p = (c+R-1)/2, u = ((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(2c). And v = (Δ, -u(Δ+1)/b). So det = (x_O - p) * (-u(Δ+1)/b) - (y_O + b(c-p)/u) * Δ. That is the cross product (O-H) × v. But we already derived a simplified expression for det in terms of c,R,b. That expression is:

det = c(c+R+1)/(2b(R-1)) + b/2 * [ (R+1)(c+R-1)(cR -3c + R^2 -2R +1)/(D(R-1)) - (R-1) ].

Now, we need to compute |O-B|^2 * Q. Let's compute |O-B|^2 first using the coordinates of O and B. But we need to express x_O and y_O in terms of c,R,b. We have:

x_O = (R(c+R+1)B1)/(4bΔ). B1 = 2b - α u (Δ+1)/b. And α = (c+R+1)/Q. So α u (Δ+1)/b = (c+R+1) u (Δ+1)/(b Q). Also, we have u(Δ+1)/b^2 = (R+1)(c+R-1)/S, where S = D = R^2 - (c-1)^2. So u(Δ+1)/b = b * (R+1)(c+R-1)/S. Thus α u (Δ+1)/b = (c+R+1) * b * (R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q). So:

x_O = R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ) * [2b - (c+R+1) b (R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)] = R(c+R+1)/(4bΔ) * b [2 - (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)] = R(c+R+1)/(4Δ) * [2 - (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)].

Now, Δ = (R-1)(c+R+1)/(2c). So 1/(4Δ) = c/(2(R-1)(c+R+1)). Thus:

x_O = R(c+R+1) * c/(2(R-1)(c+R+1)) * [2 - (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)] = R c/(2(R-1)) * [2 - (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)].

So x_O = R c/(R-1) - R c/(2(R-1)) * (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q).

Now, y_O = -R(c+R+1)^2/(4b Q). And we also have b^2 = S * ((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2) = S * (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2). So b = sqrt(S * (c+1-R)(c+1+R))/(2c). That is messy.

Now, O-B = (x_O - a, y_O + b). a = (c^2+1-R^2)/(2c). So x_O - a = R c/(R-1) - a - [R c/(2(R-1)) * (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)]. But note that R c/(R-1) - a =? Compute a = (c^2+1-R^2)/(2c). So R c/(R-1) - a = (R c/(R-1)) - ((c^2+1-R^2)/(2c)) = common denominator 2c(R-1): = (2R c^2 - (R-1)(c^2+1-R^2)) / (2c(R-1)). Expand (R-1)(c^2+1-R^2) = (R-1)c^2 + (R-1)(1-R^2) = (R-1)c^2 + (R-1)(1-R^2). Note that (1-R^2) = -(R^2-1). So (R-1)(1-R^2) = -(R-1)(R^2-1) = -(R-1)(R-1)(R+1) = -(R-1)^2 (R+1). So (R-1)(c^2+1-R^2) = (R-1)c^2 - (R-1)^2 (R+1). Then numerator: 2R c^2 - (R-1)c^2 + (R-1)^2 (R+1) = (2R - (R-1)) c^2 + (R-1)^2 (R+1) = (R+1) c^2 + (R-1)^2 (R+1) = (R+1)(c^2 + (R-1)^2). So x_O - a = (R+1)(c^2 + (R-1)^2) / (2c(R-1)). That is a nice expression! So the first term of x_O - a simplifies to that.

Now, the second term: - [R c/(2(R-1)) * (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(S Q)]. So x_O - a = (R+1)(c^2+(R-1)^2)/(2c(R-1)) - R c (c+R+1)(R+1)(c+R-1)/(2(R-1) S Q). Factor (R+1)/(2(R-1)) maybe: = (R+1)/(2(R-1)) * [ (c^2+(R-1)^2)/c - R c (c+R+1)(c+R-1)/S Q ].

Now, y_O + b = -R(c+R+1)^2/(4b Q) + b = b [1 - R(c+R+1)^2/(4b^2 Q)]. And b^2 = S ((c+1)^2 - R^2)/(4c^2) = S (c+1-R)(c+1+R)/(4c^2). So 4b^2 Q = S (c+1-R)(c+1+R) Q / c^2. Thus R(c+R+1)^2/(4b^2 Q) = R(c+R+1)^2 * c^2 / (S (c+1-R)(c+1+R) Q) = R c^2 (c+R+1)^2 / (S (c+1-R)(c+1+R) Q). So y_O + b = b [1 - R c^2 (c+R+1)^2 / (S (c+1-R)(c+1+R) Q) ].

Now, we need |O-B|^2 = (x_O - a)^2 + (y_O + b)^2. This is messy. But we also have Q. And we need to show that det^2 = |O-B|^2 * Q. That is a polynomial identity in c,R. Since we already have a simplified expression for det in terms of c,R,b, we can square it and substitute b^2. And we can also compute |O-B|^2 * Q by expressing everything in terms of c,R and b^2, and then show they are equal. This is heavy but possible with systematic simplification.

Let's try to use vector geometry with coordinates but with a different parameterization: Let’s set up coordinates so that the line MN is the x-axis, M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d>0. Now, define points C and D: C = (-r, 0), D = (d+R, 0). Then P = circumcenter of ACD. As before, P = (p, y_P) where p = (C+D)/2 = (d+R-r)/2, and y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0.

Now, define line AP. We want to find E and F. There is a nice property: For any line through A, the product of distances from A to the second intersections with the circles is related to the power of A with respect to something? But we can compute using coordinates: The parametric line AP: (x0, y0) + t * (P - A) = (x0 + t Δ, y0 + t y_P) where Δ = p - x0, and y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0. So direction v = (Δ, y_P). Note that y_P is negative.

Now, the second intersection E with Ω: x^2+y^2=r^2. Plug point: (x0+tΔ)^2 + (y0+t y_P)^2 = r^2. Since x0^2+y0^2=r^2, we get 2t(x0Δ + y0 y_P) + t^2(Δ^2+y_P^2) = 0 => t=0 or t = -2(x0Δ + y0 y_P)/(Δ^2+y_P^2). So t_E = -2(x0Δ + y0 y_P)/Q where Q = |v|^2. Now compute x0Δ + y0 y_P. Δ = p-x0, y_P = -p(x0+r)/y0. So x0Δ + y0 y_P = x0(p-x0) + y0 * (-p(x0+r)/y0) = x0p - x0^2 - p(x0+r) = x0p - x0^2 - p x0 - p r = -x0^2 - p r. So x0Δ + y0 y_P = -(x0^2 + p r). Then t_E = -2(-(x0^2+pr))/Q = 2(x0^2+pr)/Q.

Given the time, I think I will try to present a solution using complex numbers with the following approach: Represent points as complex numbers. Let M=0, N=c real. Let the circles be |z|=r, |z-c|=R. Let A = a+ib, B = a-ib. Let C = -r, D = c+R. Then P = circumcenter of A, C, D. Since C and D are real, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through p = (C+D)/2 = (c+R-r)/2. Actually we have y_P - b = -p(a+r)/b - b = -(p(a+r)+b^2)/b = -(p(a+r)+(r^2-a^2))/b = -(a+r)(p+r)/b? Because p(a+r)+r^2-a^2 = (a+r)(p+r) - 2a? Let's compute: (a+r)(p+r) = a p + a r + r p + r^2. So p(a+r)+r^2-a^2 = a p + r p + r^2 - a^2. Not equal to (a+r)(p+r) unless a^2? Actually, (a+r)(p+r) = a p + a r + r p + r^2. So difference: (a+r)(p+r) - (p(a+r)+r^2-a^2) = a p + a r + r p + r^2 - a p - r p - r^2 + a^2 = a r + a^2 = a(a+r). So p(a+r)+r^2-a^2 = (a+r)(p+r) - a(a+r) = (a+r)(p+r - a) = (a+r)(Δ). So y_P - b = - (a+r)Δ / b. Good! So v = Δ - i (a+r)Δ / b = Δ (1 - i (a+r)/b). That is a nice simplification: v = Δ * (1 - i u/b) where u = a+r. So v is a real multiple of (1 - i u/b). So the direction of AP is along (1 - i u/b). That is a complex number with argument -arctan(u/b). So AP is a line through A in that direction.

Now, E = second intersection of AP with Ω: |z|=r. Since A is on Ω, we have E = A + t_E v, with t_E real and t_E ≠ 0. As before, t_E = -2 Re(A conj(v))/|v|^2. Compute A conj(v). A = a+ib. v = Δ (1 - i u/b). So conj(v) = Δ (1 + i u/b). So A conj(v) = Δ (a+ib)(1 + i u/b) = Δ [ a + i a u/b + i b - u] = Δ [ a - u + i( a u/b + b) ]. But u = a+r, so a - u = -r. So A conj(v) = Δ [ -r + i( a u/b + b) ]. The real part is -rΔ. So Re(A conj(v)) = -rΔ. Then t_E = -2 (-rΔ)/|v|^2 = 2rΔ/|v|^2. But we also have |v|^2 = Δ^2 (1 + u^2/b^2). So t_E = 2rΔ / (Δ^2 (1+u^2/b^2)) = 2r/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2)). That's one expression.

But earlier we derived t_E = 2(r^2+pr)/Q. With p = (c+R-r)/2, and Δ = p-a. So there is consistency.

Now, F = second intersection of AP with Γ: |z-c|=R. Since A is on Γ, we have F = A + t_F v, with t_F = -2 Re((A-c) conj(v))/|v|^2. Compute (A-c) = (a-c)+ib. Then (A-c) conj(v) = Δ ((a-c)+ib)(1 + i u/b) = Δ [ (a-c) + i (a-c) u/b + i b - u] = Δ [ (a-c) - u + i( (a-c) u/b + b ) ]. But u = a+r, so (a-c)-u = a-c-a-r = -c-r. So real part = Δ(-c-r). So Re((A-c) conj(v)) = -Δ(c+r). Then t_F = -2 * (-Δ(c+r))/|v|^2 = 2Δ(c+r)/|v|^2. And |v|^2 = Δ^2 (1+u^2/b^2), so t_F = 2(c+r)/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2)). So t_F = 2(c+r)/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2)). That's nice.

Now, we have E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. And B = a - ib.

Now, we need O = circumcenter of B, E, F. Let's compute O using complex numbers. Since E and F are on the line through A with direction v, we can write E = A + α v, F = A + β v, where α = t_E, β = t_F. But note that v is proportional to (1 - i u/b). So we can set v = Δ w, where w = 1 - i u/b. Then α = t_E = 2r/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2))? Actually we have t_E = 2r/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2)). So α v = (2r/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2))) * (Δ w) = (2r/(1+u^2/b^2)) w. So E = A + (2r/(1+u^2/b^2)) w. Similarly, β v = (2(c+r)/(Δ (1+u^2/b^2))) * (Δ w) = (2(c+r)/(1+u^2/b^2)) w. So F = A + (2(c+r)/(1+u^2/b^2)) w. So we can write:

E = A + k w, where k = 2r/(1+u^2/b^2).
F = A + l w, where l = 2(c+r)/(1+u^2/b^2).

Thus E and F are on the line through A in direction w. So w = 1 - i u/b is a complex number.

Now, B = a - i b = conj(A). So we have three points: conj(A), A + k w, A + l w.

Now, we want to find the circumcenter O of these three points. Since A is on the circles, we can use the fact that the circumcircle of triangle BEF might be related to the circle with diameter something. Maybe we can find that O is the midpoint of the segment joining the orthocenter of triangle PMN and something. But we have expressions in terms of a,b,c,r.

Let’s define:

A = a + i b.
w = 1 - i u/b, where u = a+r.
Then k = 2r/(1+u^2/b^2). Note that 1+u^2/b^2 = (b^2+u^2)/b^2. So k = 2r b^2/(b^2+u^2). Similarly, l = 2(c+r) b^2/(b^2+u^2).

Thus E = A + (2r b^2/(b^2+u^2)) w, F = A + (2(c+r) b^2/(b^2+u^2)) w.

Now, w = 1 - i u/b. So we can compute E and F explicitly.

Now, we want O. There is a known formula for circumcenter of three points: O = ( |z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2) ) / ( z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) ). Let's try to compute using this.

Let z1 = B = a - i b.
z2 = E = A + k w.
z3 = F = A + l w.

We need to compute differences. Let’s denote:

z2 - z3 = (k - l) w.
z3 - z1 = (A + l w) - (a - i b) = (a + i b + l w) - (a - i b) = i b + i b? Actually, a + i b - (a - i b) = 2 i b. So z3 - z1 = 2 i b + l w.
z1 - z2 = (a - i b) - (A + k w) = (a - i b) - (a + i b) - k w = -2 i b - k w.

Now, compute denominator:

Denom = z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2) = z1 (k - l) w + z2 (2 i b + l w) + z3 (-2 i b - k w).

Plug z2 = A + k w, z3 = A + l w.

Denom = z1 (k - l) w + (A + k w)(2 i b + l w) + (A + l w)(-2 i b - k w).

Expand the last two:

(A + k w)(2 i b + l w) = A (2 i b + l w) + k w (2 i b + l w) = 2 i b A + l A w + 2 i b k w + k l w^2.

Similarly, (A + l w)(-2 i b - k w) = A(-2 i b - k w) + l w(-2 i b - k w) = -2 i b A - k A w - 2 i b l w - k l w^2.

Add these two: (2 i b A - 2 i b A) + (l A w - k A w) + (2 i b k w - 2 i b l w) + (k l w^2 - k l w^2) = A w (l - k) + 2 i b w (k - l) = (l - k)(A w - 2 i b w) = (l - k) w (A - 2 i b).

Now, note that A = a + i b, so A - 2 i b = a + i b - 2 i b = a - i b = conj(A) = z1. So A - 2 i b = z1. Thus the sum of the two expansions is (l - k) w z1.

Then add the first term: Denom = z1 (k - l) w + (l - k) w z1 = (k - l) w z1 + (l - k) w z1 = 0. So denominator is zero! That suggests that the three points are collinear? But they are not; we have B, E, F not collinear. So there must be an error: The formula for circumcenter denominator is the complex number z1(z2 - z3) + z2(z3 - z1) + z3(z1 - z2). For three points, this denominator is 2i times the signed area? Actually, it's known that this denominator is 2i times the oriented area of the triangle. It should be nonzero if points are non-collinear. So why did we get zero? Because we used z1 = a - i b, z2 = A + k w, z3 = A + l w. But note that w = 1 - i u/b, and we have relationships. Thus the simplification v = Δ (1 - i u/b) is incorrect. Let's recompute v correctly:

v = (p-a, y_P - b) = (Δ, -p(a+r)/b - b) = (Δ, -(p(a+r)+b^2)/b). So v = (Δ, -D/b) where D = p(a+r)+b^2.

So v = (Δ, -D/b). That is not a multiple of (1 - i u/b) unless D = uΔ. So we need to compute D in terms of a,b,c,r.

We have p = (c+R-r)/2, a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). And b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So D = p(a+r)+b^2 = p(a+r)+(r^2-a^2) = r^2 + p(a+r) - a^2. We can try to simplify D.

Maybe we can find that D = (a+r)(p+r-a)? Let's compute (a+r)(p+r-a) = (a+r)(p-a+r) = (a+r)(Δ+r) = (a+r)Δ + (a+r)r. That is not equal to D. So no.

Given the confusion, I think it's better to abandon the complex approach and stick with a straightforward coordinate proof using the expressions we have for t_E and t_F in terms of Δ and Q. Since we have a nice parametric form for E and F: E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v, with t_E = 2(r^2+pr)/Q, t_F = 2(a^2 - a d + p(d+r))/Q? Actually we have t_F = 2(a^2 - a d + p(d+r))/Q. But we can also compute t_F using the chord property: t_F = 2Δ(c+r)/|v|^2? That might be simpler.

Let's try to compute O using the formula for circumcenter but we need to handle the denominator not zero. We can try to compute O as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF. Since we have coordinates, we can set up equations.

Let’s use coordinates with M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Let’s denote:

a = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d)
b = √(r^2-a^2) > 0

p = (d+R-r)/2
y_P = -p(a+r)/b

Define Δ = p - a
Define D = p(a+r)+b^2

Then v = (Δ, -D/b)

Q = |v|^2 = Δ^2 + D^2/b^2

Now, t_E = 2(r^2+pr)/Q? Actually from earlier, A·v = aΔ + b(-D/b) = aΔ - D. And we computed aΔ - D = -(r^2+pr) because D = p(a+r)+b^2 = p(a+r)+(r^2-a^2) = r^2 + p(a+r) - a^2. And aΔ = a(p-a) = a p - a^2. So aΔ - D = a p - a^2 - r^2 - p(a+r) + a^2 = a p - r^2 - p a - p r = -r^2 - p r. So A·v = -(r^2+pr). Good. So t_E = -2(A·v)/Q = 2(r^2+pr)/Q.

Similarly, for Γ: (A-d, b)·v = (a-d)Δ + b(-D/b) = (a-d)Δ - D. Compute (a-d)Δ - D = (a-d)(p-a) - [p(a+r)+b^2] = (a-d)p - (a-d)a - p(a+r) - b^2 = p a - p d - a^2 + a d - p a - p r - b^2 = -p d + a d - p r - b^2 = a d - p(d+r) - b^2. So (A-d, b)·v = a d - p(d+r) - b^2. Then t_F = -2(a d - p(d+r) - b^2)/Q = 2(p(d+r) + b^2 - a d)/Q.

So we have:

E = A + (2(r^2+pr)/Q) v
F = A + (2(p(d+r) + b^2 - a d)/Q) v

Now, B = (a, -b)

We want to show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. We have P = (p, y_P), M=(0,0), N=(d,0). The altitude from P is vertical line x=p. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN: from P(p, y_P) to N(d,0) = (d-p, -y_P). So a direction vector for PN is (d-p, -y_P). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, so its direction is (y_P, d-p) (since dot: (d-p)*y_P + (-y_P)*(d-p)=0). So the line through M with direction (y_P, d-p) has equation: (x,y) = t (y_P, d-p). Intersection with x=p gives t y_P = p => t = p/y_P. Since y_P negative, t negative. Then y = t (d-p) = p(d-p)/y_P. So H = (p, p(d-p)/y_P). Note that y_P = -p(a+r)/b, so p(d-p)/y_P = p(d-p)/(-p(a+r)/b) = -b(d-p)/(a+r). So H = (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)). That matches earlier.

Now, line L through H parallel to v: direction v = (Δ, -D/b). So L: (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)) + s (Δ, -D/b).

We need to show that the distance from O to L equals radius r_BEF = distance from O to B.

Now, we have O = circumcenter of B, E, F. We can try to compute O by solving the system:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2.

Since E and F are on line through A in direction v, we can write E = A + α v, F = A + β v, with α = t_E, β = t_F. So we have:

|O - B|^2 = |O - A - α v|^2 = |O - A|^2 - 2α (O-A)·v + α^2 |v|^2. Similarly for β.

Thus we have:

|O - B|^2 = |O - A|^2 - 2α (O-A)·v + α^2 Q. (1)
|O - B|^2 = |O - A|^2 - 2β (O-A)·v + β^2 Q. (2)

Subtract (1) - (2): 0 = -2α (O-A)·v + α^2 Q + 2β (O-A)·v - β^2 Q => 2(β-α)(O-A)·v = (α^2 - β^2) Q => 2(β-α)(O-A)·v = (α-β)(α+β) Q => (β-α) [2(O-A)·v + (α+β) Q] = 0? Actually, divide both sides by (β-α) (nonzero because α≠β generally): 2(O-A)·v = (α+β) Q. So:

(O - A)·v = (α+β) Q / 2. (3)

Thus the projection of (O-A) onto v is known. That is a relation.

Also, from (1): |O - B|^2 = |O - A|^2 - 2α (O-A)·v + α^2 Q = |O - A|^2 - α (α+β) Q + α^2 Q = |O - A|^2 - αβ Q. So:

|O - B|^2 = |O - A|^2 - αβ Q. (4)

Similarly, we could get other relations.

Now, we also have that O is equidistant from B and E, so:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - A|^2 - 2α (O-A)·v + α^2 Q, which we used.

Now, we need to find O in terms of coordinates. Possibly we can find that O lies on the line through the midpoint of AB perpendicular to something? Note that B = (a, -b), A = (a, b). So AB is vertical line x=a. The midpoint of AB is (a, 0). The line through that midpoint perpendicular to v might be relevant.

Since we have (O-A)·v = (α+β) Q/2, we can write O = A + λ v + μ v_perp, where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v. Then (O-A)·v = λ Q, so λ = (α+β)/2. So O = A + ((α+β)/2) v + μ v_perp.

Thus O lies on a line through A in direction v (since the component along v is fixed) but with an additional perpendicular component that is free. So O is on a line parallel to v? Actually, O = A + ((α+β)/2) v + μ v_perp, which is a line through the point A + ((α+β)/2) v in direction v_perp. So the locus of O as μ varies is a line perpendicular to v. That line is the perpendicular bisector of segment between the two points A+α v and A+β v? Actually, the points E and F are A+α v and A+β v. The midpoint of EF is A + ((α+β)/2) v. So that line is the perpendicular bisector of EF. So indeed, O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is a line perpendicular to v through the midpoint of EF. That is a known fact: The circumcenter of BEF must lie on the perpendicular bisector of EF. So O is on that line. And we also have that O is equidistant from B and E, so O lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE. So O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF (a line perpendicular to v through midpoint of EF) and the perpendicular bisector of BE. So we can find O by solving these two linear equations.

Thus we can set up:

Let M_EF = A + ((α+β)/2) v. The perpendicular bisector of EF is the line: (X - M_EF) · v = 0. So X satisfies: (X - M_EF) · v = 0.

Similarly, the perpendicular bisector of BE: let M_BE = (B + E)/2. The line: (X - M_BE) · (E - B) = 0.

Thus O is the unique point satisfying both.

Now, we have v = (Δ, -D/b). And we have α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q, β = 2(p(d+r) + b^2 - a d)/Q.

We can compute α+β and αβ. Maybe these simplify using the relations.

Let’s compute α+β and αβ in terms of known.

First, we have:

α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q
β = 2(p(d+r) + b^2 - a d)/Q

So α+β = 2[ r^2+pr + p(d+r) + b^2 - a d ]/Q = 2[ r^2 + pr + p d + p r + b^2 - a d ]/Q = 2[ r^2 + p d + 2p r + b^2 - a d ]/Q.

But note that b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So r^2 + b^2 = 2r^2 - a^2. So α+β = 2[ 2r^2 - a^2 + p d + 2p r - a d ]/Q = 2[ 2r^2 + p d + 2p r - a^2 - a d ]/Q.

Now, p d + 2p r = p(d+2r). So α+β = 2[ 2r^2 + p(d+2r) - a(a+d) ]/Q.

We can try to simplify using a and p.

Similarly, αβ = 4(r^2+pr)(p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q^2.

Now, we also have that (O - A) = ((α+β)/2) v + μ v_perp. So the distance from O to B can be expressed in terms of μ.

But perhaps we can find that the line L through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the distance from H to the line through O perpendicular to v (which is the perpendicular bisector of EF) is something. Since L is parallel to v, its distance to O is |(O - H) × v|/|v|. And we need to show that this equals |O-B|.

Maybe we can compute |O-B|^2 using the expression for O and B, and show that |O-B|^2 * Q = |(O-H) × v|^2. This is a polynomial identity that can be verified by substituting the expressions for a,b,p, etc., and using the fact that a^2+b^2=r^2. Given the algebraic complexity, but we can attempt to do it with systematic simplification.

Since we already have a numeric example that works, and the problem is from an olympiad, there must be a synthetic solution that is not too heavy. Let's try to find a synthetic proof by considering the circle with diameter PH and the orthocenter H.

Observe that H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So PH ⟂ MN. Also, the reflection of H across MN lies on the circumcircle of PMN. Let's denote H' = reflection of H across MN. Then H' lies on circle (PMN). Also, note that A, C, D are points on circles. Maybe we can relate A to H'.

Given the time, I think I will try to produce a coordinate proof that is rigorous and present it in a clean way. I will use the following steps:

1. Set up coordinate system: M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0. Let radii r and R with r<R. Then A=(a,b), B=(a,-b) with a=(d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d), b=√(r^2-a^2).
2. Compute C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). Then P=(p, y_P) with p=(d+R-r)/2, y_P=-p(a+r)/b.
3. Let v=(Δ, y_P-b) = (p-a, -p(a+r)/b - b) = (Δ, -D/b) where D=p(a+r)+b^2.
4. Compute Q=|v|^2=Δ^2+D^2/b^2.
5. Compute t_E=2(r^2+pr)/Q, t_F=2(p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q.
6. Then E=A+t_E v, F=A+t_F v.
7. Compute H=(p, p(d-p)/y_P) = (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)).
8. Let L be line through H with direction v: param (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)) + s v.
9. We need to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). So we will compute the distance from O (circumcenter of BEF) to L and show it equals the radius.
10. To find O, we solve the system: |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2. Since E and F are on line through A with direction v, we can use the fact that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is the line through M_EF = A+((t_E+t_F)/2) v and perpendicular to v. So O = M_EF + μ v_perp, where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v.
11. Also, from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we get (O - (B+E)/2)·(E-B)=0, which is another linear equation. We can solve for μ and O.
12. After solving, we can compute the cross product (O-H) × v and the distance |O-B| and show they satisfy the equality.
13. The algebra simplifies using the relations a^2+b^2=r^2, and the expressions for a and p in terms of d,r,R.

Given the complexity, but we can try to compute O explicitly using the linear equations.

Let's define:

M_EF = A + ((t_E+t_F)/2) v.
v_perp = (D/b, Δ) (since dot with v = Δ*(D/b) + (-D/b)*Δ = 0). Actually, v = (Δ, -D/b). A vector perpendicular is (D/b, Δ) because dot = Δ*(D/b) + (-D/b)*Δ = 0. So v_perp = (D/b, Δ). So O = M_EF + μ (D/b, Δ).

Now, we also have that O satisfies the perpendicular bisector of BE: (O - (B+E)/2) · (E - B) = 0.

Compute E - B = (A + t_E v - (a, -b)) = (a + t_E Δ - a, b + t_E (-D/b) + b) = (t_E Δ, 2b - t_E D/b). So E - B = (t_E Δ, 2b - t_E D/b). Similarly, B+E = (a + (a + t_E Δ), -b + (b + t_E (-D/b))) = (2a + t_E Δ, t_E (-D/b)). So (B+E)/2 = (a + (t_E Δ)/2, - (t_E D)/(2b)). So O - (B+E)/2 = (M_EF + μ (D/b, Δ)) - (a + (t_E Δ)/2, - (t_E D)/(2b)).

Now, M_EF = A + ((t_E+t_F)/2) v = (a, b) + ((t_E+t_F)/2)(Δ, -D/b) = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)Δ, b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b)). So O = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)Δ + μ (D/b), b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + μ Δ.

Then O - (B+E)/2 = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)Δ + μ D/b - a - (t_E Δ)/2, b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + μ Δ - (- (t_E D)/(2b))? Actually, careful: The y-coordinate of (B+E)/2 is - (t_E D)/(2b). So we have:

x_O - (B+E)/2_x = ((t_E+t_F)/2)Δ + μ D/b - (t_E Δ)/2 = (t_F Δ)/2 + μ D/b.
y_O - (B+E)/2_y = b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + μ Δ + (t_E D)/(2b) = b + μ Δ + [ - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + (t_E D)/(2b) ] = b + μ Δ + [ (-t_E - t_F + t_E) D/(2b) ] = b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b).

Thus O - (B+E)/2 = ( (t_F Δ)/2 + μ D/b, b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b) ).

Now, the condition (O - (B+E)/2) · (E - B) = 0.

Compute E - B = (t_E Δ, 2b - t_E D/b).

So dot = ( (t_F Δ)/2 + μ D/b ) * (t_E Δ) + ( b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b) ) * (2b - t_E D/b) = 0.

Let's expand:

First term: t_E Δ * (t_F Δ)/2 + t_E Δ * μ D/b = (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 + μ t_E D Δ / b.

Second term: (b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b)) * (2b - t_E D/b) = (b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b)) * (2b) - (b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b)) * (t_E D/b) = 2b(b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b)) - (t_E D/b)(b + μ Δ - (t_F D)/(2b)) = 2b^2 + 2b μ Δ - t_F D - (t_E D/b) b - (t_E D/b) μ Δ + (t_E D/b)(t_F D)/(2b) = 2b^2 + 2b μ Δ - t_F D - t_E D - (t_E D μ Δ)/b + (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2).

So the sum of the two terms is:

[ (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 + μ t_E D Δ / b ] + [ 2b^2 + 2b μ Δ - t_F D - t_E D - (t_E D μ Δ)/b + (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2) ] = 0.

Combine terms:

Constant terms (no μ): (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 + 2b^2 - t_F D - t_E D + (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2).

μ terms: μ t_E D Δ / b + 2b μ Δ - (t_E D μ Δ)/b = μ Δ ( t_E D / b + 2b - t_E D / b ) = 2b μ Δ.

So we have:

2b^2 + (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 - t_F D - t_E D + (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2) + 2b μ Δ = 0.

Thus:

2b μ Δ = -2b^2 - (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 + t_F D + t_E D - (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2).

So μ = [ -2b^2 - (t_E t_F Δ^2)/2 + t_F D + t_E D - (t_E t_F D^2)/(2b^2) ] / (2b Δ).

Now, we can compute t_E and t_F in terms of Δ, D, b, etc. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for μ. Note that we have also that O = M_EF + μ v_perp, and we can compute |O-B|^2.

Now, the cross product (O-H) × v. Since v = (Δ, -D/b), and H = (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)). We can compute O-H. But perhaps we can use the condition that L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to that circle equals the square of the distance from H to L? Not.

Now, x_O - p = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)Δ + μ D/b) - p.
y_O - y_H = (b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + μ Δ) - (-b(d-p)/(a+r)) = b - ((t_E+t_F)/2)(D/b) + μ Δ + b(d-p)/(a+r).

Now, the problem statement likely expects a solution that works for all configurations. So the analytic approach should yield that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle BEF. Given the algebra, we can try to compute the condition using the coordinates we have, but we need to be careful with the algebra.

Given the time, I think I will present a solution that uses coordinate geometry and algebraic simplification, but I will present it in a clear, step-by-step manner, using the expressions for t_E and t_F and then solving for O using the linear equations. I'll try to show that the distance from O to L equals the radius. I'll use the fact that the cross product (O-H) × v can be expressed in terms of t_E and t_F and α, β, etc., and that after substitution, it simplifies to something like (t_E - t_F) times something, and that |O-B|^2 * Q equals that squared. I'll need to do the algebra carefully.

Let me try to compute O using the linear equations directly, but maybe we can find a simpler expression for O.

We have B = (a, -b), A = (a, b), v = (Δ, -D/b). Let’s denote:

E = A + α v, F = A + β v, with α = t_E, β = t_F.

We know that O satisfies: (O - (B+E)/2) · (E - B) = 0 and (O - (B+F)/2) · (F - B) = 0.

But perhaps we can find O as the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BE. The perpendicular bisector of EF is the line: (X - M_EF) · v = 0, where M_EF = A + ((α+β)/2) v.

The perpendicular bisector of BE is: (X - (B+E)/2) · (E - B) = 0.

We can solve these two linear equations for X. Write X = (x,y). Then:

(1) (x - (a + ((α+β)/2)Δ), y - (b - ((α+β)/2)(D/b))) · (Δ, -D/b) = 0 => Δ(x - a - ((α+β)/2)Δ) - (D/b)(y - b + ((α+β)/2)(D/b)) = 0 => Δ x - Δ a - ((α+β)/2)Δ^2 - (D/b) y + (D/b)b - ((α+β)/2)(D^2/b^2) = 0 => Δ x - (D/b) y - Δ a - ((α+β)/2)Δ^2 + (D/b)b - ((α+β)/2)(D^2/b^2) = 0 => Δ x - (D/b) y = Δ a + ((α+β)/2)(Δ^2 + D^2/b^2) - (D/b)b = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b.

Thus equation (1): Δ x - (D/b) y = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b.

Now, equation (2): (x - (a + (αΔ)/2), y - (-b + (α(-D/b))/2?) Actually, B+E = (a + a + αΔ, -b + b + α(-D/b)) = (2a + αΔ, α(-D/b)). So (B+E)/2 = (a + (αΔ)/2, - (αD)/(2b)). So (X - (B+E)/2) · (E - B) = 0. E - B = (αΔ, 2b - αD/b). So dot: (x - a - (αΔ)/2, y + (αD)/(2b)) · (αΔ, 2b - αD/b) = 0 => αΔ(x - a - (αΔ)/2) + (2b - αD/b)(y + (αD)/(2b)) = 0 => αΔ x - αΔ a - (α^2 Δ^2)/2 + (2b - αD/b) y + (2b - αD/b)(αD)/(2b) = 0 => αΔ x + (2b - αD/b) y = αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 - (2b - αD/b)(αD)/(2b).

Simplify the constant: (2b - αD/b)(αD)/(2b) = αD/(2b) * (2b - αD/b) = αD/(2b)*2b - αD/(2b)*αD/b = αD - α^2 D^2/(2b^2). So the right side becomes: αΔ a + (α^2 Δ^2)/2 - αD + α^2 D^2/(2b^2) = α(Δ a - D) + (α^2/2)(Δ^2 + D^2/b^2) = α(Δ a - D) + (α^2/2) Q.

But Δ a - D = a(p-a) - [p(a+r)+b^2] = a p - a^2 - p a - p r - b^2 = -a^2 - p r - b^2 = -(a^2+b^2+pr) = -(r^2+pr). So α(Δ a - D) = -α(r^2+pr). And we have α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q. So α(r^2+pr) = 2(r^2+pr)^2/Q. Thus α(Δ a - D) = -2(r^2+pr)^2/Q. So the right side becomes: -2(r^2+pr)^2/Q + (α^2/2) Q. And α^2/2 = (4(r^2+pr)^2/Q^2)/2 = 2(r^2+pr)^2/Q^2. So (α^2/2) Q = 2(r^2+pr)^2/Q. Thus the right side is: -2(r^2+pr)^2/Q + 2(r^2+pr)^2/Q = 0. So equation (2) becomes: αΔ x + (2b - αD/b) y = 0. So we have:

(2) αΔ x + (2b - αD/b) y = 0.

Now, we also have equation (1). So we have a linear system:

Δ x - (D/b) y = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b.   (1)
αΔ x + (2b - αD/b) y = 0.   (2)

We can solve for x and y. Note that α, β are constants. This is a 2x2 system. Let's denote:

A = Δ, B = -D/b, C = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b.

Then (1): A x + B y = C.

(2): (αΔ) x + (2b - αD/b) y = 0.

We can solve by Cramer's rule. The determinant D = A*(2b - αD/b) - B*(αΔ) = Δ(2b - αD/b) - (-D/b)(αΔ) = Δ(2b - αD/b) + (αDΔ)/b = Δ(2b - αD/b + αD/b) = 2b Δ.

So D = 2b Δ. Good.

Now, x = (C*(2b - αD/b) - B*0)/D = C*(2b - αD/b)/(2bΔ).

Similarly, y = (A*0 - C*(αΔ))/D = -C αΔ/(2bΔ) = -C α/(2b).

Thus we have:

x_O = C * (2b - αD/b) / (2bΔ)
y_O = - C α / (2b)

Now, we can compute C explicitly.

C = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b.

Now, we also have α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q, β = 2(p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q. And we have expressions for Δ, D, Q, etc., in terms of a,b,d,r,R.

But maybe we can find a simpler expression for C. Let's try to express C in terms of known.

Note that (α+β)/2 = [2(r^2+pr)/Q + 2(p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q]/2 = (r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q = (r^2+pr + p d + p r + b^2 - a d)/Q = (r^2 + p d + 2p r + b^2 - a d)/Q.

Now, Q = Δ^2 + D^2/b^2. Also, D = p(a+r)+b^2.

We also have that Δ = p - a.

We might find that (α+β)/2 Q simplifies to something like? Possibly (α+β)/2 Q = (r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q * Q = r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d. So ((α+β)/2) Q = r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d.

Thus C = Δ a + ((α+β)/2) Q - (D/b)b = Δ a + (r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d) - (D/b)b.

Now, Δ a = a(p-a) = a p - a^2. So C = a p - a^2 + r^2+pr + p d + p r + b^2 - a d - (D/b)b.

But b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So r^2 + b^2 = 2r^2 - a^2. So:

C = a p - a^2 + (2r^2 - a^2) + p d + p r + p r - a d - (D/b)b? Wait, we have r^2+pr + p(d+r)+b^2 - a d = r^2+pr + p d + p r + b^2 - a d = r^2 + 2pr + p d + b^2 - a d = (r^2+b^2) + 2pr + p d - a d = (2r^2 - a^2) + 2pr + p d - a d. Then add Δ a = a p - a^2. So total = a p - a^2 + (2r^2 - a^2) + 2pr + p d - a d = a p + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr + p d - a d. Then subtract (D/b)b.

Thus C = a p + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr + p d - a d - (D/b)b.

Now, note that D = p(a+r)+b^2 = p a + p r + b^2. So (D/b)b = D^2/b^2? Actually (D/b)b = D. So (D/b)b = D. That is simple! Because (D/b)b = D. So we have:

C = a p + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr + p d - a d - D.

Now, D = p a + p r + b^2. So:

C = a p + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr + p d - a d - (p a + p r + b^2) = a p + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr + p d - a d - p a - p r - b^2 = (a p - p a) + 2r^2 - 2a^2 + 2pr - p r + p d - a d - b^2 = 2r^2 - 2a^2 + pr + p d - a d - b^2.

But b^2 = r^2 - a^2, so 2r^2 - 2a^2 - b^2 = 2r^2 - 2a^2 - (r^2 - a^2) = r^2 - a^2 = b^2. So:

C = b^2 + pr + p d - a d = b^2 + p(d+r) - a d.

Thus C = b^2 + p(d+r) - a d.

Now, recall that we earlier had t_F = 2(p(d+r)+b^2 - a d)/Q = 2C/Q? Actually, p(d+r)+b^2 - a d = C. So t_F = 2C/Q. And we also have α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q. So C is related to t_F.

Thus we have:

C = b^2 + p(d+r) - a d.

Now, we also have from the expression for y_O: y_O = - C α/(2b). And α = 2(r^2+pr)/Q. So y_O = - C * (2(r^2+pr)/Q) / (2b) = - C (r^2+pr)/(b Q).

Similarly, x_O = C * (2b - αD/b) / (2bΔ). Compute 2b - αD/b = 2b - (2(r^2+pr)/Q) * D/b = 2b - (2(r^2+pr)D)/(b Q). So:

x_O = C * [ 2b - (2(r^2+pr)D)/(b Q) ] / (2bΔ) = C * [ 2b - (2(r^2+pr)D)/(b Q) ] / (2bΔ) = C * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] / Δ.

Thus x_O = C/Δ * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ].

Now, we have O = (x_O, y_O). B = (a, -b). So we can compute |O-B|^2.

But maybe we can also compute the cross product det(O-H, v) and show it equals |O-B| * |v|.

Now, H = (p, -b(d-p)/(a+r)). Let’s denote H = (p, -b(d-p)/u) with u=a+r.

Now, v = (Δ, -D/b). So we need to compute det = (x_O - p)*(-D/b) - (y_O + b(d-p)/u)*Δ.

We already derived an expression for det in terms of μ and Q. But perhaps we can compute det directly using the expressions for x_O and y_O.

Let’s compute x_O - p and y_O + b(d-p)/u.

We have x_O = C/Δ * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ]. And p = (d+R-r)/2. Also, we have C = b^2 + p(d+r) - a d. And we have relationships.

We also have y_O = - C (r^2+pr)/(b Q). So y_O + b(d-p)/u = - C (r^2+pr)/(b Q) + b(d-p)/u.

Now, det = - (D/b)(x_O - p) - Δ(y_O + b(d-p)/u). So det = - (D/b)(x_O - p) - Δ y_O - Δ b(d-p)/u.

But note that - Δ y_O = - Δ * ( - C (r^2+pr)/(b Q) ) = Δ C (r^2+pr)/(b Q). So det = - (D/b)(x_O - p) + Δ C (r^2+pr)/(b Q) - Δ b(d-p)/u.

Now, x_O - p = C/Δ * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] - p. So - (D/b)(x_O - p) = - (D/b)(C/Δ * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] - p) = - (D C)/(b Δ) * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] + (D p)/b.

Then det = - (D C)/(b Δ) * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] + (D p)/b + Δ C (r^2+pr)/(b Q) - Δ b(d-p)/u.

Now, note that the term - (D C)/(b Δ) * [ 1 - ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) ] = - (D C)/(b Δ) + (D C)/(b Δ) * ((r^2+pr)D)/(b^2 Q) = - (D C)/(b Δ) + (D^2 C (r^2+pr))/(b^3 Δ Q). And then we have + Δ C (r^2+pr)/(b Q) = C Δ (r^2+pr)/(b Q). So the sum of these two terms is: - (D C)/(b Δ) + (D^2 C (r^2+pr))/(b^3 Δ Q) + C Δ (r^2+pr)/(b Q). We can find perpendicular bisectors. Let's denote coordinates.

Let’s compute P = (x_P, y_P). Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at midpoint of CD: midpoint of C and D is ((-r + d+R)/2, 0). Now we also need P equidistant to A and C (or A and D). So we can compute y_P by solving distance squared to A equals distance squared to C.

Let’s compute:

x_P = (d+R - r)/2.

Let’s denote:

C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0), A = (x0, y0).

Set PA^2 = PC^2.

Compute PC^2: (x_P - (-r))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Compute PA^2: (x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2.

Set equal:

(x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

(x_P^2 - 2x_P x0 + x0^2) + (y_P^2 - 2y_P y0 + y0^2) = (x_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2) + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2:

-2x_P x0 + x0^2 - 2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Bring terms:

-2x_P x0 - 2y_P y0 + (x0^2+y0^2) = 2r x_P + r^2.

But note: x0^2 + y0^2 = r^2 (since A lies on Ω). So x0^2+y0^2 = r^2.

Thus:

-2x_P x0 - 2y_P y0 + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2 => Cancel r^2:

-2x_P x0 - 2y_P y0 = 2r x_P => Divide 2: -x_P x0 - y_P y0 = r x_P => So:

y_P y0 = -x_P x0 - r x_P = -x_P (x0 + r).

Thus:

y_P = -x_P (x0 + r) / y0.

Now x_P = (d+R - r)/2. So we can compute y_P. Since y0 > 0, we need sign: y_P may be negative? Let's see: x_P positive? d+R - r > 0 because R > r and d>0, so x_P > 0. x0 may be positive or negative? For intersecting circles, x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Since r < R, we have R^2 - r^2 > 0. So x0 could be positive if d^2 > R^2 - r^2? Actually d^2 + r^2 - R^2 can be positive, zero, or negative. If circles intersect, the chord AB is perpendicular to MN at point (x0,0). Typically, if d^2 > R^2 - r^2, then x0 > 0; if d^2 = R^2 - r^2, then x0=0; if d^2 < R^2 - r^2, then x0<0. But the order C-M-N-D: M=0, N=d>0. So C=-r<0, D=d+R>0. So A is somewhere; its x-coordinate x0 could be positive, negative, or zero. Now y_P = -x_P (x0+r)/y0. So sign: if x0+r >0, then y_P negative because -x_P * positive / positive = negative. So y_P <0. If x0+r <0, then y_P positive? Actually -x_P * negative = positive.
